Welcome to the command line!¶

Lets go over some basic commands
ls lists everything in the current directory -- try it

In [1]:
# just type ls and hit enter
# or you can cut and paste this whole block of text
# the '#' symbols make these lines into comments
ls

Desktop    Downloads                                Music     Public     Videos
Documents  Lgn_nod_double_BASH_CSHL_20191016.ipynb  Pictures  Templates

cd lets you change the current directory -- try it!

In [2]:
# change directory to 'Desktop'
cd Desktop

What is in the "Desktop" directory?

???

In [3]:
# we can use 'relative paths' to quickly move up, out of the Desktop directory
cd .. # moves up one level
In [4]:
# we can use 'absolute paths' to go to a precise location
cd /scratch # move to a folder that has a full address of "/scratch"
In [ ]:
# By default in CyVerse Atmosphere, 
# we need to change the ownership of the /scratch drive 
# to use as our project space
# we can use ls to see the current ownership

ls -l / #option '-l' lists more information
# current ownership is 'root'
In [6]:
# We need special privileges to change the ownership
# we can use the 'super user do', sudo command to access these privileges
# You will be prompted for your CyVerse password

sudo chown $(whoami) /scratch

[sudo] password for sal269:

did it work?
how can we check?

In [8]:
# To copy the data required for this tutorial
# we will need to use a set of software tools called IRODS.
# This seems somewhat unique to CyVerse -- and it is annoying

# initialize your connection to the CyVerse datastore

iinit

Enter your current iRODS password:

follow the prompts and enter:
1) your cyverse password
2) host = data.cyverse.org
3) port = 1247
4) zone = iplant

In [10]:
# when your iRODS connection is set up,
# get the data needed for the course

# iRODS command iget
# options are -r for directories and contents
#             -P for a progress update
#             -K for checksums to see that file was transferred completely
#             -N 4 to use 4 CPUs
iget -rPKf -N 4 /iplant/home/sal269/CSHL_Cereal_Genomics_2019/course_materials

In [15]:
# not easy to tell from just listing them
# we can use a special unix character called a pipe "|"
# to pass our ls output to another command wc, which can count words

ls ./raw_reads | wc
# output is #lines #words #bytes

     52      52    1585

Using the tools that introduced so far,
can you determine how many reads are in WT10_S8_L001_R1_001.fastq?

Try it out!

RNAseq alignment and read counting¶

We will move on to installing and using command line tools
There's lots more to learn about the unix command line
Here's a cool book if you want to know more http://vincebuffalo.org/book/

Command line tools often have complicated dependances,
sometimes the software developers aren't even aware!

To reduce the complexity, we will use a package manager called conda.
By making environments with explicit lists of softwares,
conda makes it easy to install what we will need.

In [22]:
# But first, we need to install conda

# use wget to download miniconda from the internet
wget https://repo.continuum.io/miniconda/Miniconda3-latest-Linux-x86_64.sh

#run the installation script
bash Miniconda3-latest-Linux-x86_64.sh
# press enter, scroll down and say 'yes'
# accept the defaults and say yes again

In [5]:
# Create new conda environment for RNAseq alignment and counting
# the environment is named 'alignment'
# and contains the 3.6 release of python
# the most up-to-date version of fastp for quality control
#                             samtools for general read handling
#                               hisat2 for spliced alignment
#                          and subread for read counting

conda create -y --name alignment python=3.6 fastp samtools hisat2 subread

In [13]:



    


#Use fastp to look at your sequence files and verify they are ok
#If Illumina adapter sequences are detected at the end of your read, fastp will remove them
#for single end fastp -i in.R1.fq.gz -o out.R1.fq.gz 

#run fastp in command line
mkdir ./fastp_report   #reports go here
mkdir ./trimmed_reads  #trimmed reads go here

# this is a more complicated command
# here, we use the command 'find' to identify all files with same ending name
# and process them one by one -- 'do'-ing the middle stuff
# until there are no more files and we are 'done'

# in the middle, we are looking at the raw reads with fastp
find ./raw_reads -name "*_L001_R1_001.fastq" | while read f; do
STEM=$(basename "${f}" _L001_R1_001.fastq)
fastp -w 4 -i ./raw_reads/${STEM}_L001_R1_001.fastq \
    -o ./trimmed_reads/${STEM}.fastq.gz \
    -h ./fastp_report/${STEM}.html \
    -j ./fastp_report/${STEM}.json \
    -R "${STEM} fastp report"
done

# use HiSAT2 to align your reads to the genome
# HiSAT2 is a fast, splicing aware aligner that can accept cDNA inputs

# lets try running HiSAT2 on just one file
# but we will cancel it after just a few seconds
# use control+c to terminate the file after you have seen its output
# how do we determine the options to use?

hisat2 --min-intronlen 60 --max-intronlen 50000 \
    -p 4 -x ./maize_genome/ZmaysAGPv3.30.hisat2 \
    -U ./trimmed_reads/WT10_S8.fastq.gz
    
# this will output a SAM file directly to the command line

In [23]:



    


# use HiSAT2 to align your reads to the genome
# HiSAT2 is a fast, splicing aware aligner that can accept cDNA inputs

mkdir ./aligned_reads # make destination folder
mkdir ./HiSAT2_report # just for summary stats

#min intron 60 max intron 50000 from Chen et al. 2018
#running in unstranded mode - input files with -U
#output to command line then pipe to samtools view
#and capture stdout with > to make a BAM file
#capture stderr with 2> to make a report file
find ./trimmed_reads -name "*.fastq.gz" | while read f; do
	STEM=$(basename "${f}" .fastq.gz)
	hisat2 --min-intronlen 60 --max-intronlen 50000 \
	-p 4 -x ./maize_genome/ZmaysAGPv3.30.hisat2 \
	-U ./trimmed_reads/"${STEM}".fastq.gz \
    2> ./HiSAT2_report/"${STEM}"_summary.txt \
    | samtools view -bS - \
    > ./aligned_reads/"${STEM}".bam
done

## use featureCounts to get expression values
# count by EXON and
# summarize by GENE
# this is an UNION-EXON approach

#command template
#featureCounts -p -a annotation.gtf -t exon -g gene id
#-o counts.txt mapping results PE.bam

mkdir ./featureCounts

for f in ./aligned_reads/*.bam; do
  STEM=$(basename "${f}" .bam)
  featureCounts -T 4 \
      -a ./maize_genome/Zea_mays.AGPv3.30.repaired.gtf \
      -t exon -g gene_id \
      -o ./featureCounts/${STEM}.counts.txt \
      ./aligned_reads/${STEM}.bam
done
